					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Neo-Aramaic dialect</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about one of the modern dialect groups spoken by Assyrians. For similarly named languages, see <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_language" class="mw-disambig" title="Assyrian language">Assyrian language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Hatran_Aramaic" title="Hatran Aramaic">Hatran Aramaic</a>.</div>

languages">Afro-Asiatic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">Northeastern</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>Suret</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Early forms</div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><div style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Proto-Afroasiatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Afroasiatic">Proto-Afroasiatic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em; padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Proto-Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Semitic">Proto-Semitic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:0.45em; padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic" title="Old Aramaic">Old Aramaic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:0.45em; padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Middle_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Aramaic">Middle Aramaic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:0.45em; padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Middle_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Middle Aramaic">Eastern Middle Aramaic</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:0.45em; padding-left:0"><li><a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">Northeastern Neo-Aramaic</a>

</li></ul>
</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Dialects</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Christian_Neo-Aramaic_dialect_of_Urmia" title="Christian Neo-Aramaic dialect of Urmia">Christian Urmi Neo-Aramaic</a>, including Urban Koine; <a href="/wiki/Nochiya_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Nochiya tribe">Nōčiyay</a>; Hakkari groups (<a href="/wiki/Barwari" title="Barwari">Barwari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jilu" title="Jilu">Jilu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">Tyari</a>); Southern (<a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh</a>); others</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">Writing system</a></span></div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist nowrap">

<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyriansample.ogg" title="File:Assyriansample.ogg">Spoken Assyrian Neo-Aramaic</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="215" style="width:215px;" data-durationhint="61" data-mwtitle="Assyriansample.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Assyriansample.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/1d/Assyriansample.ogg/Assyriansample.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">The Urmi dialect, spoken by TV presenter Maryam Shamalta.</div></div></div></div>
</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below noprint selfref" style="background-color:#E7E7FF;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Suret</b> (<a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>: <span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܣܘܪܝܬ</span>) (<a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Help:IPA for Aramaic">[ˈsu:rɪtʰ]</a> or <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Help:IPA for Aramaic">[ˈsu:rɪθ]</a>), also known as <b>Assyrian</b><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> refers to the varieties of <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">Northeastern Neo-Aramaic</a> (NENA) spoken by Christians, namely <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nordhoff,_Sebastian_2013_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nordhoff,_Sebastian_2013-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The various NENA dialects descend from <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic" title="Old Aramaic">Old Aramaic</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> in the later phase of the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian Empire</a>, which slowly displaced the <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">East Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> beginning around the 10th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer198644_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer198644-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bae,_C_2004_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bae,_C_2004-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> They have been further heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Classical Syriac</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic#Middle_Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Middle Aramaic</a> dialect of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a>, after its adoption as an official <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac churches</a>, but Suret is not a direct descendant of Classical Syriac.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Suret speakers are indigenous to <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a>, northwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, southeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, which is a large region stretching from the plain of <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> through to the <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh Plains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erbil_Governorate" title="Erbil Governorate">Erbil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk_Governorate" title="Kirkuk Governorate">Kirkuk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dohuk_Governorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Dohuk Governorate">Duhok</a> regions in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, together with the northerneastern regions of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and to southcentral and southeastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maclean_1895_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maclean_1895-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Instability throughout the Middle East over the past century has led to a <a href="/wiki/Assyrian%E2%80%93Chaldean%E2%80%93Syriac_diaspora" title="Assyrian–Chaldean–Syriac diaspora">worldwide diaspora of Suret speakers</a>, with most speakers now living abroad in such places as North and South America, Australia, Europe and Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Speakers of Suret and <a href="/wiki/Turoyo_language" title="Turoyo language">Turoyo (Surayt)</a> are ethnic Assyrians and are the descendants of the ancient inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">SIL</a> distinguishes between Chaldean and Assyrian as varieties of Suret on non-<a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistic</a> grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Suret is mutually intelligible with some NENA dialects spoken by Jews, especially in the western part of its historical extent.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Its mutual intelligibility with Turoyo is partial and asymmetrical, but more significant in written form.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khan_2008,_pp._6_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan_2008,_pp._6-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Suret is a moderately-<a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflected</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional language</a> with a two-<a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> noun system and rather flexible <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Khan_2008,_pp._6_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan_2008,_pp._6-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> There is some <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> influence on the language.<sup id="cite_ref-:Khan_2007_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Khan_2007-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In its native region, speakers may use <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> loanwords, while diaspora communities may use loanwords borrowed from the languages of their respective countries. Suret is written from <a href="/wiki/Right-to-left_script" title="Right-to-left script">right-to-left</a> and it uses the <i>Madnḥāyā</i> version of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Suret, alongside other modern Aramaic languages, is now considered <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">endangered</a>, as newer generation of Assyrians tend to <a href="/wiki/Language_attrition" title="Language attrition">not acquire the full language</a>, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acculturation" title="Acculturation">acculturation</a> into their new resident countries.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG/180px-P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="135" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG/270px-P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG/360px-P1050578_Louvre_Ob%C3%A9lisque_de_Manishtusu_d%C3%A9tail_rwk.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Manishtushu_Obelisk" class="mw-redirect" title="Manishtushu Obelisk">Manishtushu Obelisk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> (detail). The obelisk was erected by <a href="/wiki/Manishtushu" title="Manishtushu">Manishtushu</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon the Great</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>) circa 2270-2255 BC<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> have been in extensive contact since their old periods. Local unwritten Aramaic dialects emerged from <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Aramaic" title="Imperial Aramaic">Imperial Aramaic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>. In around 700 BC, Aramaic slowly started to replace Akkadian in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. Widespread bilingualism among Assyrian nationals was already present prior to the fall of the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Simo_Parpola_Assyrian_Identity_in_Ancient_Times_and_Today_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simo_Parpola_Assyrian_Identity_in_Ancient_Times_and_Today-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The language transition was achievable because the two languages featured similarities in grammar and vocabulary, and because the 22-lettered <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a> was simpler to learn than the <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">Akkadian cuneiform</a> which had over 600 signs.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The converging process that took place between Assyrian Akkadian and Aramaic across all aspects of both languages and societies is known as <i>Aramaic-Assyrian symbiosis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Introduced as the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian Empire</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_III" title="Tiglath-Pileser III">Tiglath-Pileser III</a> (745–727<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BC), it became the language of commerce and trade, the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular language</a> of Assyria in the late <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-imperial_aramaic_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imperial_aramaic-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_British_Survey_1968,_page_3_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_British_Survey_1968,_page_3-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–605 BC), <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> (605–539 BC), the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (539–323 BC), the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> (247 BC–224 AD) and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> (224–651 AD). Following the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid</a> conquest of Assyria under <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> language was adopted as the "vehicle for written communication between the different regions of the vast empire with its different peoples and languages". After the conquest of Assyria by the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> in the late 4th century BC, Imperial Aramaic gradually lost its status as an imperial language, but continued to flourish alongside <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/250px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/375px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg/500px-Syriac_Sert%C3%A2_book_script.jpg 2x" data-file-width="991" data-file-height="748" /></a><figcaption>An 11th-century <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Classical Syriac</a> <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscript</a>, written in <i>Serto</i> script</figcaption></figure>
<p>By the 1st century AD, Akkadian was extinct, though vocabulary and grammatical features still survive in modern NENA dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaufman_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">Neo-Aramaic languages</a> evolved from <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Middle Syriac-Aramaic</a> by the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Iranica_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence that the drive for the adoption of Syriac was led by missionaries. Much literary effort was put into the production of an authoritative translation of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> into Syriac, the <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> (<span title="Syriac-language text"><span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܦܫܝܛܬܐ</span></span>, <i><span title="Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syr-Latn">Pšīṭtā</i></span></i>). At the same time, <a href="/wiki/Ephrem_the_Syrian" title="Ephrem the Syrian">Ephrem the Syrian</a> was producing the most treasured collection of poetry and theology in the Classical Syriac language.
</p><p>By the 3rd century AD, churches in <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Urhay</a> in the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a> began to use Classical Syriac as the language of worship and it became the literary and liturgical language of many churches in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>. Syriac was the common tongue of the region, where it was the native language of the Fertile Crescent, surrounding areas, as well as in parts of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a>. It was the dominant language until 900 AD, till it was supplanted by Greek and later Arabic in a centuries-long process having begun in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Arab conquests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg/220px-Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg/330px-Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg/440px-Assyrian_Gospel_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="725" data-file-height="539" /></a><figcaption>An 18th-century gospel Book from the <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The differences with the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> led to the bitter <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_schism" title="Nestorian schism">Nestorian schism</a> in the Syriac-speaking world. As a result of the schism as well as being split between living in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in the west and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> in the east, Syrian-Aramaic developed distinctive <a href="/wiki/Turoyo_language" title="Turoyo language">Western</a> and Eastern varieties. Although remaining a single language with a high level of comprehension between the varieties, the two employ distinctive variations in pronunciation and writing systems and, to a lesser degree, in vocabulary and grammar. During the course of the third and fourth centuries, the inhabitants of the region began to embrace Christianity. Because of theological differences, Syriac-speaking Christians bifurcated during the fifth century into the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a>, or <a href="/wiki/East_Syriac_Rite" title="East Syriac Rite">East Syriac Rite</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Orthodox">Syriac Orthodox</a>, or <a href="/wiki/West_Syriac_Rite" title="West Syriac Rite">West Syriac Rite</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. After this separation, the two groups developed distinct dialects differing primarily in the pronunciation and written symbolisation of <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer198644_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer198644-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bae,_C_2004_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bae,_C_2004-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christianity.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The distribution of the Syriac language in the Middle East and Asia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/295px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="295" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/443px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/590px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1273" data-file-height="685" /></a><figcaption>Post 2010, in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, Chaldean Neo-Aramaic is mainly spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh Plains</a> and the cities around <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duhok" title="Duhok">Duhok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a> (magenta).</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_the_Levant" title="Mongol invasions of the Levant">Mongol invasions of the Levant</a> in the 13th century and the religiously motivated massacres of Assyrians by <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> further contributed to the rapid decline of the language. In many places outside of northern Mesopotamia, even in <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgy</a>, the language was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> "Modern Syriac-Aramaic" is a term occasionally used to refer to the modern Neo-Aramaic languages spoken by Christians, including Suret. Even if they cannot be positively identified as the direct descendants of <a href="/wiki/Attested_language" title="Attested language">attested</a> Middle Syriac, they must have developed from closely related dialects belonging to the same branch of Aramaic, and the varieties spoken in Christian communities have long co-existed with and been influenced by Middle Syriac as a liturgical and literary language. Moreover, the name "Syriac", when used with no qualification, generally refers to one specific dialect of Middle Aramaic but not to Old Aramaic or to the various present-day Eastern and <a href="/wiki/Central_Neo-Aramaic" title="Central Neo-Aramaic">Central Neo-Aramaic</a> languages descended from it or from close relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-lipinski_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lipinski-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, the <i>Constitution of the Iraqi Kurdistan Region</i> recognized Syriac in article 7, section four, stating, "Syriac shall be the language of education and culture for those who speak it in addition to the Kurdish language."<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_of_the_Iraqi_Kurdistan_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution_of_the_Iraqi_Kurdistan-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iraq" title="Constitution of Iraq">Constitution of Iraq</a> recognised it as one of the "official languages in the administrative units in which they constitute density of population" in article 4, section four.<sup id="cite_ref-Iraqi_constitution_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iraqi_constitution-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Iraq&#39;s_constitution_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iraq&#39;s_constitution-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Script">Script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Script"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_papyri.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Syriac_papyri.jpg/150px-Syriac_papyri.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="218" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Syriac_papyri.jpg/225px-Syriac_papyri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Syriac_papyri.jpg/300px-Syriac_papyri.jpg 2x" data-file-width="488" data-file-height="709" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">Papyrus</a> fragment of the 9th century written in <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet#West_Syriac_Serṭā" title="Syriac alphabet">Serto</a> variant. A passage from the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a> is recognizable</figcaption></figure>
<p>The original <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> writing system, believed to be the world's oldest, was derived around 3600 BC from this method of keeping accounts. By the end of the 4th millennium BC, the Mesopotamians were using a triangular-shaped stylus made from a reed pressed into soft clay to record numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Around 2700 BC, <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> began to represent syllables of spoken <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a> genetically unrelated to the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a> that it neighboured. About that time, Mesopotamian cuneiform became a general purpose writing system for <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> and numbers. This script was adapted to another Mesopotamian language, the <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">East Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_period" title="Old Assyrian period">Assyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a>) around 2600 BC.
</p><p>With the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–609<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BC), Old Aramaic was also adapted to Mesopotamian cuneiform. The last cuneiform scripts in Akkadian discovered thus far date from the 1st century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Various bronze lion-weights found in <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> featured both the Akkadian and Aramaic text etched on them, bearing the names of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_kings" title="List of Assyrian kings">Assyrian kings</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">Shalmaneser III</a> (858-824 B.C), <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">King Sargon</a> (721-705 B.C) and <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">Sennacherib</a> (704-681 B.C). Indication of contemporaneous existence of the two languages in 4th century B.C. is present in an Aramaic document from <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a> written in cuneiform. In <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, Akkadian writing vanished by 140 B.C, with the exclusion of a few priests who used it for religious matters. Though it still continued to be employed for astronomical texts up until the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">common era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Syriac script is a <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> primarily used to write the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a> from the 1st century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjads</a> directly descending from the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a> and shares similarities with the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic</a> and the traditional <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian alphabets</a>. The alphabet consists of 22 letters, all of which are consonants. It is a <a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">cursive</a> script where some, but not all, letters connect within a word.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Aramaic writing has been found as far north as <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Prehistoric Britain</a>, in the form of inscriptions in Aramaic, made by Assyrian soldiers serving in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">Roman Legions</a> in northern England during the 2nd<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_development">Modern development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg/250px-East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg/375px-East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg/500px-East_Syriac_Script_Thaksa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="386" /></a><figcaption>Classical Syriac written in <i>Madnhāyā</i> script. <a href="/wiki/Thrissur" title="Thrissur">Thrissur</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, 1799</figcaption></figure>
<p>The oldest and classical form of the alphabet is <i><span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">ʾEsṭrangēlā</i></span></i> (<span title="Syriac-language text"><span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܐܣܛܪܢܓܠܐ</span></span>); the name is thought to derive from the Greek adjective <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">στρογγύλη</span></span> (<i>strongúlē</i>) 'round'.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Although ʾEsṭrangēlā is no longer used as the main script for writing Syriac, it has undergone some revival since the 10th century.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> gradually began to be the dominant spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> after the 7th century AD, texts were often written in Arabic with the Syriac script. <a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a> was also written with Syriac script and was called <a href="/wiki/Suriyani_Malayalam" title="Suriyani Malayalam">Suriyani Malayalam</a>. Such non-Syriac languages written in Syriac script are called <i><a href="/wiki/Garshuni" title="Garshuni">Garshuni</a></i> or <i>Karshuni</i>.
</p><p>The <i>Madnhāyā</i>, or 'eastern', version formed as a form of shorthand developed from ʾEsṭrangēlā and progressed further as handwriting patterns changed. The <i>Madnhāyā</i> version also possesses optional vowel markings to help pronounce Syriac. Other names for the script include <i><span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">Swāḏāyā</i></span></i>, 'conversational', often translated as "contemporary", reflecting its use in writing modern Neo-Aramaic.
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:6thBeatitude.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/6thBeatitude.svg/400px-6thBeatitude.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="23" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/6thBeatitude.svg/600px-6thBeatitude.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/6thBeatitude.svg/800px-6thBeatitude.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1496" data-file-height="86" /></a><figcaption>The sixth <a href="/wiki/Beatitudes" title="Beatitudes">beatitude</a> (<a href="/wiki/Matthew_5:8" title="Matthew 5:8">Matthew 5:8</a>) in Classical Syriac from the Peshitta (in <i>Madnhāyā</i>):<br /><style ܕܐܵܢܝܼ ܒܸܬ ܚܵܙܝܼ ܠܐܲܠܵܗܵܐ.</span>&#8206;<br /><i><span title="Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syr-Latn">Ṭūḇā l-ʾānī d-ʾīnā diḵyē b-libbā, sābāb d-ʾānī bit xāzī l-ʾalāhā</i></span>.</i><br />'Blessed are the pure in heart, for they shall see God.'</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Letters"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table style="border: #ccf 1px solid; margin-left:0.5em; float: right; clear:right;" dir="rtl">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="background:lavender;text-align:center;" dir="ltr"><b><a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet</a></b><br /><small>(<i>200 BCE–present</i>)</small>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="center" style="padding-left:3px; padding-right:3px;"><a href="/wiki/Aleph" title="Aleph">ܐ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Bet_(letter)" title="Bet (letter)">ܒ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Gimel" title="Gimel">ܓ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Dalet" title="Dalet">ܕ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/He_(letter)" title="He (letter)">ܗ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Waw_(letter)" title="Waw (letter)">ܘ</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="center" style="padding-left:3px; padding-right:3px;"><a href="/wiki/Zayin" title="Zayin">ܙ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Heth" title="Heth">ܚ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Teth" title="Teth">ܛ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Yodh" title="Yodh">ܝ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Kaph" title="Kaph">ܟܟ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Lamedh" title="Lamedh">ܠ</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="center" style="padding-left:3px; padding-right:3px;"><a href="/wiki/Mem" title="Mem">ܡܡ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Nun_(letter)" title="Nun (letter)">ܢܢ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Samekh" title="Samekh">ܣ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">ܥ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Pe_(Semitic_letter)" title="Pe (Semitic letter)">ܦ</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="center" style="padding-left:3px; padding-right:3px;"><a href="/wiki/Tsade" title="Tsade">ܨ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Qoph" title="Qoph">ܩ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Resh" title="Resh">ܪ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Shin_(letter)" title="Shin (letter)">ܫ</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Taw" title="Taw">ܬ</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Three letters act as <a href="/wiki/Mater_lectionis" title="Mater lectionis">matres lectionis</a>: rather than being a consonant, they indicate a vowel. <i><a href="/wiki/Aleph_(letter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aleph (letter)">ʾĀlep̄</a></i> (<span title="Syriac-language text"><span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܐ</span></span>), the first letter, represents a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>, but it can also indicate the presence of certain vowels (typically at the beginning or the end of a word, but also in the middle). The letter <i><a href="/wiki/Waw_(letter)" title="Waw (letter)">Waw</a></i> (<span title="Syriac-language text"><span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܘ</span></span>) is the consonant <i>w</i>, but can also represent the vowels <i>o</i> and <i>u</i>. Likewise, the letter <span class="nowrap"><i><a href="/wiki/Yodh" title="Yodh">Yōḏ</a></i> (<span title="Syriac-language text"><span lang="syr" dir="rtl">ܝ</span></span>)</span> represents the consonant <i>y</i>, but it also stands for the vowels <i>i</i> and <i>e</i>. In addition to foreign sounds, a marking system is used to distinguish <i><span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">qūššāyā</i></span></i> ('hard' letters) from <i><span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">rūkkāḵā</i></span></i> ('soft' letters). The letters <i>Bēṯ</i>, <i>Gāmal</i>, <i>Dālaṯ</i>, <i>Kāp̄</i>, <i>Pē</i> and <i>Taw</i>, all <a href="/wiki/Plosives" class="mw-redirect" title="Plosives">plosives</a> ('hard'), are able to be <a href="/wiki/Spirantization" class="mw-redirect" title="Spirantization">spirantised</a> into <a href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">fricatives</a> ('soft').
</p><p>The system involves placing a single dot underneath the letter to give its 'soft' variant and a dot above the letter to give its 'hard' variant (though, in modern usage, no mark at all is usually used to indicate the 'hard' value).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_alphabet">Latin alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Latin alphabet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet#Latin_alphabet_and_romanization" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet §&#160;Latin alphabet and romanization</a></div>
<p>In the 1930s, following the state policy for minority languages of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> was developed and some material published.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrock198911–23_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrock198911–23-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Despite the fact that this innovation did not displace the Syriac script, the usage of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> in the Assyrian community has become rather widespread due to the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian%E2%80%93Chaldean%E2%80%93Syriac_diaspora" title="Assyrian–Chaldean–Syriac diaspora">Assyrian diaspora</a>'s predominant settlement in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a>, where the Latin script dominates.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Latin alphabet is preferred by most Assyrians for practical reasons and its convenience, especially in <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>, where it is used to communicate. Although the Syriac Latin alphabet contains <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritics</a>, most Assyrians rarely utilise the modified letters and would conveniently rely on the <a href="/wiki/ISO_basic_Latin_alphabet" title="ISO basic Latin alphabet">basic Latin alphabet</a>. The Latin alphabet is also a useful tool to present Assyrian terminology to anyone who is not familiar with the Syriac script. A precise <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">transcription</a> may not be necessary for native Suret speakers, as they would be able to pronounce words correctly, but it can be very helpful for those not quite familiar with Syriac and more informed with the Latin script.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</td>
<td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Notes:</b>
</p>
<ul><li>In all NENA dialects, voiced, voiceless, aspirated and emphatic consonants are recognised as distinct phonemes, though there can be an overlap between plain voiceless and voiceless emphatic in sound quality.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brill_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harrassowitz_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrassowitz-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>In Iraqi Koine and many Urmian &amp; Northern dialects, the palatals [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_plosive" title="Voiceless palatal plosive">c</a></span>], [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_plosive" title="Voiced palatal plosive">ɟ</a></span>] and aspirate [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">cʰ</a></span>] are considered the predominant realisation of /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a></span>/, /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">g</a></span>/ and aspirate /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">kʰ</a></span>/.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrassowitz_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrassowitz-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brill_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the Koine and Urmi dialects, velar fricatives /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">ɣ</a></span>/ are typically uvular as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_fricative" title="Voiceless uvular fricative">χ</a></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_fricative" title="Voiced uvular fricative">ʁ</a></span>].<sup id="cite_ref-Harrassowitz_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrassowitz-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The phoneme /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiceless pharyngeal fricative">ħ</a></span>/ is in most dialects realised as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a></span>]. The one exception to this is the dialect of <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9rtevin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hértevin language">Hértevin</a>, which merged the two historical phonemes into [ħ], thus lacking [x] instead.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal</a> /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">ʕ</a></span>/, represented by the letter <i><a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">'e</a></i>, is a marginal phoneme that is generally upheld in formal or religious speech. Among the majority of Suret speakers, <a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">'<i>e</i></a> would be realised as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">aɪ̯</a></span>], [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">eɪ̯</a></span>], [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ</a></span>], [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_approximant" title="Voiced palatal approximant">j</a></span>], <a href="/wiki/Zero_(linguistics)" title="Zero (linguistics)">deleted</a>, or even <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">geminating</a> the previous consonant, depending on the dialect and phonological context.</li>
<li>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar trill">r</a></span>/ may also be heard as a tap sound [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_taps_and_flaps" title="Voiced dental and alveolar taps and flaps">ɾ</a></span>].<sup id="cite_ref-Harrassowitz_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrassowitz-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiceless labiodental fricative">f</a></span>/ is a phoneme heard in the <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">Tyari</a>, Barwari and Chaldean dialects. In most of the other varieties, it merges with /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a></span>/,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> though [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiceless labiodental fricative">f</a></span>] is found in loanwords.</li>
<li>The phonemes <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span> have allophonic realisations of [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">θ</a></span>] and [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_fricative" title="Voiced dental fricative">ð</a></span>] (respectively) in most Lower Tyari, Barwari and Chaldean dialects, which is a carryover of <i><a href="/wiki/Begadkefat" title="Begadkefat">begadkefat</a></i> from the Ancient Aramaic period.</li>
<li>In the Upper Tyari dialects, /θ/ is realised as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">ʃ</a></span>] or [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless alveolar plosive">t</a></span>]; in the Marga dialect, the /t/ may at times be replaced with [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">s</a></span>].</li>
<li>In the Urmian dialect, /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93velar_approximant" title="Voiced labial–velar approximant">w</a></span>/ has a widespread allophone [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_approximant" title="Voiced labiodental approximant">ʋ</a></span>] (it may vacillate to [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">v</a></span>] for some speakers).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the Jilu dialect, /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a></span>/ is uttered as a tense [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a></span>]. This can also occur in other dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brill_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the Iraqi Koine dialect, a labial-palatal approximant sound [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93palatal_approximant" title="Voiced labial–palatal approximant">ɥ</a></span>] is also heard.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harrassowitz_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrassowitz-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a></span>/ is affricated, thus pronounced as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a></span>] in some Urmian, Tyari and Nochiya dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a></span>/ would be affricated to [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless postalveolar affricate">t͡ʃ</a></span>] in the same process.</li>
<li>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">ɣ</a></span>/ is a marginal phoneme that occurs across all dialects. Either a result of the historic splitting of /g/, through loanwords, or by contact of [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a></span>] with a voiced consonant.</li>
<li>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a></span>/ is found predominately from loanwords, but, in some dialects, also from the voicing of /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">ʃ</a></span>/<sup id="cite_ref-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Neo-Aramaic_Dialect_of_Jilu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> (e.g. <i>ḥašbunā</i> /xaʒbu:na:/, "counting", from the root <i>ḥ-š-b,</i> "to count") as in the Jilu dialect.</li>
<li>/n/ can be pronounced [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_nasal" title="Voiced velar nasal">ŋ</a></span>] before velar consonants [x] and [q] and as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_nasal" title="Voiced bilabial nasal">m</a></span>] before labial consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In some speakers, a <a href="/wiki/Dental_click" title="Dental click">dental click</a> (English "tsk") may be used para-linguistically as a negative response to a "<a href="/wiki/Yes_and_no" title="Yes and no">yes or no</a>" question. This feature is more common among those who still live in the homeland or in the Middle East, than those living in the diaspora.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg/250px-Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="191" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg/375px-Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg/500px-Iraqi_Koine_Vowel_Trapezoid.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="921" data-file-height="702" /></a><figcaption>The vowel phonemes of Iraqi Koine</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to linguist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_Odisho&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Edward Odisho (page does not exist)">Edward Odisho</a>, there are six vowel phonemes in Iraqi Koine.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> They are as follows:
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">

<tbody><tr>
<th>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">Front</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Central_vowel" title="Central vowel">Central</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">Back</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">Close</a>
</th>
<td>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a></span>/
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a></span>/
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Half-close_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-close vowel">Half-close</a>
</th>
<td colspan="2">/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a></span>/
</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Half_open_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Half open vowel">Half-open</a>
</th>
<td>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">e</a></span>/
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td>/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">o</a></span>/
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">Open</a>
</th>
<td colspan="2">/<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">a</a></span>/
</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, as commonly uttered in words like <i>n<b>a</b>ša</i> ("man; human"), is central <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">ä</a>&#93;</span> for many speakers. It is usually <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>&#93;</span> in the Urmian and <a href="/wiki/Nochiya_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Nochiya tribe">Nochiya</a> dialects. For some Urmian and <a href="/wiki/Jilu" title="Jilu">Jilu</a> speakers, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ</a>&#93;</span> may be used instead. In those having a more pronounced Jilu dialect, this vowel is mostly fronted and <a href="/wiki/Raising_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Raising (phonetics)">raised</a> to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ</a>&#93;</span>. In the <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">Tyari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barwari" title="Barwari">Barwari</a> dialects, it is usually more back <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Long_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Long vowel">long vowel</a>, as heard in <i>r<b>a</b>ba</i> ("much; many"), may also be realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">ɒ</a>&#93;</span>, depending on the speaker. It is more rounded and higher in the Urmian dialect, where it is realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a>&#93;</span>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span>, heard in <i>b<b>e</b>ta</i> ("house") is generally diphthongised to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eɪ̯]</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Anda%C3%A7,_Uludere" title="Andaç, Uludere">Halmon</a> dialect (a Lower Tyari tribe). To note, the [aj] <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative (language)">vestigial</a> trait of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Syriac" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Syriac">classical Syriac</a> and thereby may be used in <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">formal speech</a> as well, such as in <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ/</span>, uttered in words like <i>d<b>ə</b>dwa</i> ("housefly"), is sometimes realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">ə</a>&#93;</span> (a <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a>).</li>
<li>The mid vowels, preserved in Tyari, Barwari, Baz and Chaldean dialects, are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Raising_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Raising (phonetics)">raised</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonological_change" title="Phonological change">merged</a> with close vowels in Urmian and some other dialects:
<ul><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span>, as in <i>g<b>o</b>ra</i> ("big"), is raised to [u]. The Urmian dialect may diphthongise it to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊj]</span>.</li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span>, as in <i>k<b>e</b>pa</i> ("rock"), is raised to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i]</span>.</li></ul></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span>, as in <i>t<b>o</b>ra</i> ("bull") may be diphthongised to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑw]</span> in some Tyari, Barwari, Chaldean and Jilu dialects.</li>
<li>Across many dialects, <a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">close</a> and <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">close-mid vowels</a> are <a href="/wiki/Near-close_vowel" title="Near-close vowel">lax</a> when they occur in a <a href="/wiki/Syllable#Open_and_closed" title="Syllable">closed syllable</a>:
<ul><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> is usually realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊ]</span>;</li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> is usually realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ]</span>.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>East Syriac dialects may recognize half-close sounds as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛ]</span> and also recognize the back vowel <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">ɒ</a>&#93;</span> as a long form of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two basic <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a> exist, namely <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aj/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aw/</span>. For some words, many dialects have <a href="/wiki/Monophthongization" title="Monophthongization">monophthongised</a> them to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[e]</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[o]</span> respectively. For <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">substantives</a>, A common vowel alteration is <a href="/wiki/Apophony" title="Apophony">apophonically</a> shifting the final <i>-a</i> to <i>-e</i>, so <i>ṭer<b>a</b></i> ('bird') will be <i>ṭer<b>e</b></i> ('birds') in its plural form.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetics_of_Iraqi_Koine">Phonetics of Iraqi Koine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonetics of Iraqi Koine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Iraqi Koine is a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_levelling" title="Dialect levelling">merged</a> dialect which formed in the mid-20th century, being influenced by both Urmian and Hakkari dialects.
</p>
<ul><li>Iraqi Koine, like the majority of the Suret dialects, realises <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93velar_approximant" title="Voiced labial–velar approximant">w</a>&#93;</span> instead of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_approximant" title="Voiced labiodental approximant">ʋ</a>&#93;</span>.</li>
<li>Iraqi Koine generally realises the <a href="/wiki/Interdental_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Interdental fricative">interdental fricatives</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">θ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_fricative" title="Voiced dental fricative">ð</a>/</span> in words like <i>ma<b>ṯ</b>a</i> ("village") and <i>rqa<b>ḏ</b>a</i> ("dancing") as <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_stop" title="Alveolar stop">alveolar stops</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless alveolar plosive">t</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar plosive">d</a>&#93;</span> respectively.</li>
<li>Dorsal fricatives /<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">ɣ</a></span>/ are heard as uvular as [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_fricative" title="Voiceless uvular fricative">χ</a></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_fricative" title="Voiced uvular fricative">ʁ</a></span>].</li>
<li>Predominantly, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/q/</span> in words like <i><b>q</b>alama</i> ("pen") does not merge with <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span>.</li>
<li>The diphthong <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aw/</span> in words like <i>t<b>aw</b>ra</i> ("bull"), as heard in most of <a href="/wiki/Hakkari_(historical_region)" title="Hakkari (historical region)">Hakkari</a> dialects, are realised as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">o</a>&#93;</span>: <i>t<b>o</b>ra</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊj]</span> diphthong in <i>z<b>uy</b>ze</i> ("money") is retained as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a>&#93;</span>: <i>z<b>u</b>ze</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_British_Survey_1968,_page_3_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_British_Survey_1968,_page_3-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Depending on the speaker, the <a href="/wiki/Velar_stop" title="Velar stop">velar stops</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span> may be affricated as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡ʃ]</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d͡ʒ]</span> respectively.</li>
<li>The <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡ʃ]</span> in some present progressive verbs like <i><b>č</b>i'axla</i> ("[she] eats") is retained as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>&#93;</span>: <i><b>k</b>i'axla</i>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetics_of_Chaldean-Neo-Aramaic">Phonetics of Chaldean-Neo-Aramaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonetics of Chaldean-Neo-Aramaic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</td>
<td style="border-right: 0;"></td>
<td style="border-left: 0;"><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">rˤ</a></span>
