</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>The Chaldean dialects are generally characterised by the presence of the fricatives <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> (<i>th</i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ð/</span> (<i>dh</i>) which correspond to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span>, respectively, in other Assyrian dialects (excluding the <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">Tyari</a> dialect).</li>
<li>In some Chaldean dialects <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span> is realized as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar approximant">ɹ</a>&#93;</span>. In others, it is either a <a href="/wiki/Tap_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Tap consonant">tap</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_taps_and_flaps" title="Voiced dental and alveolar taps and flaps">ɾ</a>&#93;</span> or a <a href="/wiki/Trill_consonant" title="Trill consonant">trill</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar trill">r</a>&#93;</span>.</li>
<li>Unlike in Assyrian Neo-Aramaic, the <a href="/wiki/Guttural" title="Guttural">guttural</a> sounds of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">ʕ</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiceless pharyngeal fricative">ħ</a>&#93;</span> are used predominantly in Chaldean varieties; this is a feature also seen in other <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">Northeastern Neo-Aramaic</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESara1974_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESara1974-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels_2">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

</th>

</th></tr>

<th><a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">Close</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a></span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close central unrounded vowel">ɨ</a></span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">Mid</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">e</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">o</a></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">Open</a>
</th>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">a</a></span>
</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>NENA is a <a href="/wiki/Null-subject_language" title="Null-subject language">null-subject language</a> with both <a href="/wiki/Ergative_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ergative language">ergative morphology</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Nominative-accusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative-accusative">nominative-accusative</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a>, Suret may share similar grammatical features with <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a> in the way they employ the negative <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a> in its full form before the verbal <a href="/wiki/Constituent_(linguistics)" title="Constituent (linguistics)">constituent</a> and also with the <a href="/wiki/Affirmation_and_negation" title="Affirmation and negation">negated</a> forms of the <a href="/wiki/Present_perfect" title="Present perfect">present perfect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Verbal stems<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></dt></dl>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Aspect</th>
<th>Stem
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">Imperative</a></b></td>
<td><i>ptux</i> ("open!")
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Realis_mood" title="Realis mood">Indicative</a></b></td>
<td><i>patx-</i> ( + <i>k- / ki-</i> present, <i>bit-</i> future, <i>qam-</i> past, transitive, definite object) ("opens")
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">Perfect</a></b></td>
<td><i>ptix-</i> (<a href="/wiki/Perfect_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfect participle">perfect participle</a>, f. <i>ptixta</i>, m. <i>ptixa</i>, pl. <i>ptixe</i>) ("opened")
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">Gerund</a></b></td>
<td><i>(bi-)ptaxa</i> ("opening")
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suffixes">Suffixes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Suffixes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Suret uses verbal inflections marking person and number. The suffix "<i>-e</i>" indicates a (usually masculine) <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> (i.e. <i>ward<b>a</b></i>, "flower", <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">becomes</a> <i>ward<b>e</b></i>, "flower<b>s</b>"). <a href="/wiki/Enclitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclitic">Enclitic</a> forms of <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">personal pronouns</a> are affixed to various parts of speech. As with the <a href="/wiki/Object_pronoun" title="Object pronoun">object pronoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Possessive" title="Possessive">possessive pronouns</a> are <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a> that are <a href="/wiki/Possessive_affix" title="Possessive affix">attached to the end of nouns</a> to express possession similar to the English pronouns <i>my, your, his, her,</i> etc., which reflects the gender and plurality of the person or persons.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> This is a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a> feature found in other Semitic languages and also in unrelated languages such as <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>), <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> (<a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>). Moreover, unlike many other languages, Suret has virtually no means of <a href="/wiki/Morphological_derivation" title="Morphological derivation">deriving</a> words by adding prefixes or suffixes to words. Instead, they are formed according to a limited number of templates applied to roots.<sup id="cite_ref-Solomon,_Zomaya_S.1994_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon,_Zomaya_S.1994-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Modern Assyrian, like Akkadian but unlike Arabic, has only "sound" plurals formed by means of a plural ending (i.e. no <a href="/wiki/Broken_plurals" class="mw-redirect" title="Broken plurals">broken plurals</a> formed by changing the <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stem</a>). As in all Semitic languages, some masculine nouns take the prototypically feminine plural ending (<i>-tā</i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Possessive_suffixes">Possessive suffixes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Possessive suffixes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" align="center">
<caption>Iraqi Koine possessive suffixes
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a></th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">singular</a></th>
<th>plural
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>1st person</td>
<td><i>betī</i> (my house)</td>
<td><i>betan</i> (our house)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2nd person (masc.)</td>
<td><i>betux</i> (your house)</td>
<td rowspan="2"><i>betōxun</i> (your house)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2nd person (fem.)</td>
<td><i>betax</i> (your house)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3rd person (masc.)</td>
<td><i>betū</i> (his house)</td>
<td rowspan="2"><i>betéh</i> (their house)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3rd person (fem.)</td>
<td><i>betō</i> (her house)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Although possessive suffixes are more convenient and common, they can be optional for some people and seldom used, especially among those with the Tyari and Barwari dialects, which take a more <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a> approach regarding possession, just like English <a href="/wiki/Possessive_determiner" title="Possessive determiner">possessive determiners</a>. The following are <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastic</a> ways to express possession, using the word <i>betā</i> ("house") as a base (in Urmian/Iraqi Koine):
</p>
<ul><li>my house: <i>betā-it dīyī</i> ("house-of mine")</li>
<li>your (<a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">masc.</a>, sing.) house: <i>betā-it dīyux</i> ("house-of yours")</li>
<li>your (fem., sing.) house: <i>betā-it dīyax</i> ("house-of yours")</li>
<li>your (plural) house: <i>betā-it dīyōxun</i> ("house-of yours")</li>
<li>3rd person (masc., sing.): <i>betā-it dīyū</i> ("house-of his")</li>
<li>3rd person (fem., sing.): <i>betā-it dīyō</i> ("house-of hers")</li>
<li>3rd person (plural): <i>betā-it dīyéh</i> ("house-of theirs")</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Stress"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1134653256"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132"><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></span><figcaption></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyriantone.ogg" title="File:Assyriantone.ogg"><i>dīyeh</i> ("his"), <i>dīyéh</i> ("theirs")</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="232" style="width:232px;" data-durationhint="3" data-mwtitle="Assyriantone.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b2/Assyriantone.ogg/Assyriantone.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b2/Assyriantone.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">An unstressed <i>-eh</i> denotes a singular possessive form (masc.), whereas a stressed <i>-éh</i> is a third person <a href="/wiki/Apostrophe#Plural_possessive" title="Apostrophe">plural possessive</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Like English, Suret is a <a href="/wiki/Isochrony" title="Isochrony">stress-timed language</a>, but would mostly retain unreduced vowels (as in Arabic), although some dialects may be more <a href="/wiki/Syllable_timing" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable timing">syllable-timed</a>. An example of stress timing is noticeable in the word "<i>qat</i>", an <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(grammar)" title="Conjunction (grammar)">adverb clause conjunction</a> which translates to "so that" – The 'a' sound in "<i>qat</i>" is unstressed and thus would turn into a <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a> if one would place the stress in the next word of the sentence, so; "<i>mīri qat āzekh</i>" becomes "<i>mīri qət āzekh</i>" ("I said that we go"). Another example is observed in teen <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">numerical</a> range (13-19); In some dialects, the words "<i>īštāser</i>" (sixteen) or "<i>arbāser</i>" (fourteen), among other teen numbers, the typically stressed vowel in the middle (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80" title="Ā">long A</a>) is reduced to a schwa, hence "īštəser" and "<i>arbəser</i>", respectively. In native words, Suret almost always <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stresses</a> the <a href="/wiki/Penult" title="Penult">penultimate</a> syllable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Although Suret, like all Semitic languages, is not a <a href="/wiki/Tonal_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonal language">tonal language</a>, a tonal stress is made on a plural possessive suffix -<i>éh</i> (i.e. <i>dīy<b>éh</b></i>; "their") in the final vowel to <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">tonally differentiate</a> it from an unstressed -<i>eh</i> (i.e. <i>dīyeh</i>; "his"), which is a masculine <a href="/wiki/Possessive_determiner" title="Possessive determiner">singular possessive</a>, with a standard stress pattern falling on the penult. The -<i>eh</i> used to denote a singular <a href="/wiki/Third_person_(grammar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Third person (grammar)">third person</a> masculine possessive (e.g. <i>bābeh</i>, "his father"; <i>aqleh</i>, "his leg") is present in most of the traditional dialects in <a href="/wiki/Hakkari_(historical_region)" title="Hakkari (historical region)">Hakkari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh Plains</a>, but not for Urmian and some Iraqi Koine speakers, who instead use -<i>ū</i> for possessive "his" (e.g. <i>bābū</i>, "his father"; <i>aqlū</i>, "his leg"), whilst retaining the stress in -<i>éh</i> for "their".<sup id="cite_ref-Solomon,_Zomaya_S.1994_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon,_Zomaya_S.1994-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This phenomenon however may not always be present, as some Hakkari speakers, especially those from Tyari and Barwar, would use analytic speech to denote possession. So, for instance, <i>bābeh</i> (literally, "father-his") would be uttered as <i>bābā-id dīyeh</i> (literally, "father-of his"). In Iraqi Koine and Urmian, the plural form and the third person plural possessive suffix of many words, such as <i>wardeh</i> and <i>biyyeh</i> ("flowers"/"eggs" and "their flower(s)"/"their eggs", respectively), would be <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a> were it not for the varying, distinctive stress on the penult or ultima.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Determinative">Determinative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Determinative"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When it comes to a <a href="/wiki/Determinative" title="Determinative">determinative</a> (like in English <i>this</i>, <i>a</i>, <i>the</i>, <i>few</i>, <i>any</i>, <i>which</i>, etc.), Suret generally has an absence of an <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)#Zero_article" title="Article (grammar)">article</a> (English "the<i>"</i>), unlike other Semitic languages such as <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, which does use a <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ال</span>, <i>al-</i>). <a href="/wiki/Demonstratives" class="mw-redirect" title="Demonstratives">Demonstratives</a> (<i>āhā</i>, <i>āy</i>/<i>āw</i> and <i>ayyāhā/awwāhā</i> translating to "<a href="/wiki/Demonstrative" title="Demonstrative">this</a>", "<a href="/wiki/That" title="That">that</a>" and "that one over there", respectively, demonstrating <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative#Distal_and_proximal_demonstratives" title="Demonstrative">proximal, medial and distal deixis</a>) are commonly utilised instead (e.g. <i>āhā betā</i>, "this house"), which can have the sense of "the". An indefinite article ("a(n)") can mark definiteness if the word is a <a href="/wiki/Direct_object" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct object">direct object</a> (but not a subject) by using the prepositional prefix "<i>l-</i>" paired with the proper suffix (e.g. <i>šāqil qālāmā</i>, "he takes <b>a</b> pen" vs. <i>šāqil-<b>lāh</b> qālāmā</i>, "he takes <b>the</b> pen"). <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)#Partitive_article" title="Article (grammar)">Partitive</a> articles may be used in some speech (e.g. <i>bayyīton <b>xačča</b> miyyā?</i>, which translates to "do you [pl.] want <b>some</b> water?").<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In place of a definite article, Ancient Aramaic used the emphatic state, formed by the addition of the suffix: "<i>-ā</i>" for generally masculine words and "<i>-t(h)ā</i>" (if the word already ends in <i>-ā</i>) for feminine. The definite forms were <i>pallāxā</i> for "the (male) worker" and <i>pallāxtā</i> for "the (female) worker". Beginning even in the Classical Syriac era, when the prefixed preposition "<i>d-</i>" came into more popular use and replaced state Morphology for marking possession, the emphatic (definite) form of the word became dominant and the definite sense of the word <a href="/wiki/Phonological_change" title="Phonological change">merged</a> with the indefinite sense so that <i>pālāxā</i> became "a/the (male) worker" and <i>pālaxtā</i> became "a/the (female) worker."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonantal_root">Consonantal root</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Consonantal root"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most NENA <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> and verbs are built from <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root#Triconsonantal_roots" title="Semitic root">triconsonantal roots</a>, which are a form of word formation in which the <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a> is modified and which does not involve stringing <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> together sequentially. Unlike Arabic, <a href="/wiki/Broken_plural" title="Broken plural">broken plurals</a> are not present. Semitic languages typically utilise triconsonantal roots, forming a "grid" into which vowels may be inserted without affecting the basic root.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2021)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The root <i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">š-q-l</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫ-ܩ-ܠ</span></span>) has the basic meaning of "taking", and the following are some words that can be formed from this root:
</p>
<ul><li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šqil-leh</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܩܝܼܠ ܠܹܗ</span></span>): "he has taken" (literally "taken-by him")</li>
<li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šāqil</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܵܩܸܠ</span></span>): "he takes"</li>
<li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šāqlā</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܵܩܠܵܐ</span></span>): "she takes"</li>
<li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šqul</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܩܘܿܠ</span></span>): "take!"</li>
<li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šqālā</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܩܵܠܵܐ</span></span>): "taking"</li>
<li><i><span title="Classical Syriac-language romanization"><i lang="syc-Latn">šqīlā</i></span></i> (<span title="Classical Syriac-language text"><span lang="syc" dir="rtl">ܫܩܝܼܠܵܐ</span></span>): "taken"</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenses">Tenses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tenses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Suret has lost the <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">perfect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperfect" title="Imperfect">imperfect</a> <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphological</a> tenses common in other Semitic languages. The <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present tense</a> is usually marked with the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronoun</a> followed by the <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participle</a>; however, such pronouns are usually omitted in the case of the third person. This use of the participle to mark the present tense is the most common of a number of <i>compound</i> tenses that can be used to express varying senses of tense and aspect.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2021)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Suret's new system of inflection is claimed to resemble the one of the Indo-European languages, namely the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>. This assertion is founded on
the utilisation of an <a href="/wiki/Active_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Active participle">active participle</a> concerted with a <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Passive_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive participle">passive participle</a> with a genitive/<a href="/wiki/Dative" class="mw-redirect" title="Dative">dative</a> element which is present in <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> and in Neo-Aramaic.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Modern Persian and Suret build the <a href="/wiki/Present_perfect_tense" class="mw-redirect" title="Present perfect tense">present perfect tense</a> around the past/<a href="/wiki/Resultative" title="Resultative">resultative</a> participle in conjunct with the copula (though the placing and form of the copula unveil crucial differences). The more conservative Suret dialects lay the copula in its full shape before the verbal <a href="/wiki/Constituent_(linguistics)" title="Constituent (linguistics)">constituent</a>. In the Iraqi and Iranian dialects, the previous construction is addressable with different types of the copula (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Deictic" class="mw-redirect" title="Deictic">deictic</a>) but with the elemental copula only the cliticised form is permitted. Among conservative Urmian speakers, only the construction with the enclitic ordered after the verbal constituent is allowed. Due to <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a>, the similarities between Kurdish and Modern Persian and the Urmian dialects become even more evident with their <a href="/wiki/Negation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Negation (linguistics)">negated</a> forms of present perfect, where they display close similarities.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent feature of Suret is the usage of the <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a> instead of the present base for the expression of the <a href="/wiki/Present_progressive" class="mw-redirect" title="Present progressive">present progressive</a>, which is also united with the copula. Although the language has some other varieties of the copula precedent to the verbal constituent, the common construction is with the infinitive and the basic copula cliticsed to it. In the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Neo-Aramaic_dialect_of_Urmia" title="Jewish Neo-Aramaic dialect of Urmia">Jewish Neo-Aramaic dialect of Urmia</a>, the symmetrical order of the constituents is with the present perfect tense. This structure of the NENA dialects is to be compared with the present progressive in Kurdish and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> as well, where the enclitic follows the infinitive. Such construction is present in Kurdish, where it is frequently combined with the <a href="/wiki/Locative" class="mw-redirect" title="Locative">locative</a> element "in, with", which is akin to the preposition bi- preceding the infinitive in Suret (as in "bi-ktawen" meaning 'I'm writing'). The similarities of the constituents and their <a href="/wiki/Morphosyntactic_alignment" title="Morphosyntactic alignment">alignment</a> in the present progressive construction in Suret is clearly attributed to influence from the neighbouring languages, such as the use of the infinitive for this construction and the employment of the enclitic copula after the verbal base in all verbal constructions, which is due to the impinging of the Kurdish and Turkish speech.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> and the valency of the verb, and the arrangement of the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_relation" title="Grammatical relation">grammatical roles</a> should be noticed when it comes to the similarities with <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Persian" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern Persian">Modern Persian</a> made no distinction between <a href="/wiki/Transitive_verb" title="Transitive verb">transitive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intransitive_verb" title="Intransitive verb">intransitive verbs</a>, where it unspecialised the <a href="/wiki/Absolutive" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolutive">absolutive</a> type of inflection. Different handling of inflection with transitive and intransitive verbs is also nonexistent in the NENA dialects. In contrast with Persian though, it was the ergative type that was generalised in NENA.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ergativity">Ergativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ergativity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although Aramaic has been a nominative-accusative language historically, <a href="/wiki/Split_ergativity" title="Split ergativity">split ergativity</a> in Christian and Jewish Neo-Aramaic languages developed through interaction with ergative <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a>, which is spoken by the Muslim population of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Ergativity formed in the <a href="/wiki/Perfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfective">perfective</a> aspect only (the <a href="/wiki/Imperfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperfective">imperfective</a> aspect is nominative-accusative), whereas the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a>, the original <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a> <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_construction" title="Grammatical construction">construction</a> of the passive participle, was expressed as an <a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">oblique</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative case</a>, and is presented by <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">verb-agreement</a> rather than case. The absolutive argument in <a href="/wiki/Transitive_verb" title="Transitive verb">transitive</a> clauses is the syntactic <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">object</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The dialects of Kurdish make a concordant distinction between transitive and intransitive verbs by using a tense-split ergative pattern, which is present in the tense system of some NENA dialects; The nominative accusative type is made use of in the present for all the verbs and also for intransitive verbs in past tense and the ergative type is used instead for transitive verbs.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unique among the Semitic languages, the development of ergativity in Northeastern Neo-Aramaic dialects involved the departure of original Aramaic tensed <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">finite verbal</a> forms.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Thereafter, the active participle became the root of the Suret imperfective, while the <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">passive</a> participle evolved into the Suret perfective.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2020)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Extended-Ergative dialects, which include Iraqi Koine, Hakkari and Christian Urmian dialects, show the lowest state of ergativity and would mark <a href="/wiki/Unaccusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Unaccusative">unaccusative</a> subjects and <a href="/wiki/Intransitive_verb" title="Intransitive verb">intransitive verbs</a> in an ergative pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Vocabulary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One online Suret dictionary, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.assyrianlanguages.org/sureth/index.php">Sureth Dictionary</a>, lists a total 40,642 words–half of which are <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root words</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Due to geographical proximity,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Suret <a href="/wiki/List_of_loanwords_in_Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="List of loanwords in Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">has an extensive number</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a> <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>–namely <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and Kurdish–incorporated in its vocabulary, as well as some Arabic, Russian, Azeri and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish" title="Ottoman Turkish">Ottoman Turkish</a> and, increasingly within the last century, English loanwords.
</p><p>Suret has numerous words borrowed into its vocabulary directly from Akkadian, some of them also being borrowed into neighbouring Semitic languages such as Arabic and Hebrew. Several of these words are not attested in Classical Edessan Syriac, many of them being <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> terms, being more likely to survive by being spoken in agrarian rural communities rather than the urban centres like Edessa.<sup id="cite_ref-:Khan_2007_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Khan_2007-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> A few deviations in pronunciation between the Akkadian and the Assyrian Aramaic words are probably due to mistranslations of cuneiform signs which can have several readings. While Akkadian nouns generally end in "<i>-u</i>" in the nominative case, Assyrian Neo-Aramaic words nouns end with the vowel "<i>-a</i>" in their lemma form.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_tribes" title="List of Assyrian tribes">List of Assyrian tribes</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/260px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="177" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/390px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/520px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1190" data-file-height="810" /></a><figcaption>Map of the Assyrian dialects</figcaption></figure>
<p>SIL Ethnologue distinguishes five dialect groups: Urmian, Northern, Central, Western and Sapna, each with sub-dialects. Mutual intelligibility between the Suret dialects is as high as 80%–90%.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> dialect has become the prestige dialect of Suret after 1836, when that dialect was chosen by <a href="/wiki/Justin_Perkins" title="Justin Perkins">Justin Perkins</a>, an American <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a> missionary, for the creation of a standard literary dialect. A second standard dialect derived from General Urmian known as "Iraqi <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">Koine</a>", developed in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1852, Perkins's translation of the Bible into General Urmian was published by the <a href="/wiki/American_Bible_Society" title="American Bible Society">American Bible Society</a> with a parallel text of the Classical Syriac <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grouping">Grouping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Grouping"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="36" data-mwtitle="Urmianaccent.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Urmianaccent.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Urmianaccent.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/47/Urmianaccent.ogg/Urmianaccent.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Sample of the Urmian dialect. Note the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a> influences on <a href="/wiki/Speech_tempo" title="Speech tempo">cadence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">pronunciation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYildiz2000a42_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYildiz2000a42-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> particularly the use of [v], [ʊj] and the frequency of [t͡ʃ].</figcaption></figure>
<ul><li><i><b>Iranian group</b></i>:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> (west of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Urmia" title="Lake Urmia">Lake Urmia</a>) (<a href="/wiki/Christian_Neo-Aramaic_dialect_of_Urmia" title="Christian Neo-Aramaic dialect of Urmia">Christian Neo-Aramaic dialect of Urmia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sopurghan" title="Sopurghan">Sopurghan</a> (north of <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naghadeh" class="mw-redirect" title="Naghadeh">Naghadeh</a> (south of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Urmia" title="Lake Urmia">Lake Urmia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salmas" title="Salmas">Salmas</a> (north west of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Urmia" title="Lake Urmia">Lake Urmia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sanandaj" title="Sanandaj">Sanandaj</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iranian_Kurdistan" title="Iranian Kurdistan">Iranian Kurdistan</a>) (<a href="/wiki/Christian_Neo-Aramaic_dialect_of_Senaya" title="Christian Neo-Aramaic dialect of Senaya">Senaya dialect</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li><i><b>Turkey group</b></i>:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nochiya_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Nochiya tribe">Nochiya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jilu" title="Jilu">Jilu</a> (west of Gavar and south of Qudshanis)</li>
<li>Gawar (between <a href="/wiki/Salmas" title="Salmas">Salmas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Van" title="Lake Van">Van</a>)</li>
<li>Diza</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baz,_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Baz, Turkey">Baz</a></li>
<li><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="24" data-mwtitle="Tyariaccent.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Tyariaccent.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/14/Tyariaccent.ogg/Tyariaccent.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Tyariaccent.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Sample of the Tyari dialect. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iraqi_Koine">Iraqi Koine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Iraqi Koine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_5" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" style="width:220px;" data-durationhint="23" data-mwtitle="IraqiKoine.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:IraqiKoine.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/IraqiKoine.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/77/IraqiKoine.ogg/IraqiKoine.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Sample of the Iraqi Koine dialect (voice by <a href="/wiki/Linda_George_(Assyrian_singer)" title="Linda George (Assyrian singer)">Linda George</a>). Notice how it combines the phonetic features of the Hakkari (Turkey) and Urmian (Iran) dialects</figcaption></figure>
<p>Iraqi Koine, also known as Iraqi Assyrian and "Standard" Assyrian, is a compromise between the rural Ashiret accents of Hakkari and <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh Plains</a> (listed above) and the former prestigious dialect in <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>. Iraqi Koine does not really constitute a new dialect, but an incomplete merger of dialects, with some speakers sounding more Urmian, such as those from <a href="/wiki/Habbaniyah" title="Habbaniyah">Habbaniyah</a>, and others more Hakkarian, such as those who immigrated from northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. Koine is more analogous or similar to Urmian in terms of manner of articulation, place of articulation and its <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant cluster</a> formations than it is to the Hakkari dialects, though it just lacks the regional <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> influence in some consonants and vowels, as the <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a> in Urmian tend to be more fronted and the <a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">back ones</a> more rounded.<sup id="cite_ref-Shamāshā_Dāwīd_1963,_p._895_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shamāshā_Dāwīd_1963,_p._895-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> For an <a href="/wiki/English_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="English accent">English accent</a> equivalence, the difference between Iraqi Koine and Urmian dialect would be akin to the difference between <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, many Assyrians living in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> were <a href="/wiki/Sayfo" title="Sayfo">forced from their homes</a>, and many of their descendants now live in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. The relocation has led to the creation of this dialect. Iraqi Koine was developed in the <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a> areas of Iraq (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>, Habbaniyah and <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>), which became the meccas for the rural Assyrian population. By the end of the 1950s, vast number of Assyrians started to speak Iraqi Koine. Today, Iraqi Koine is the predominant use of communication between the majority of the Assyrians from <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cities_of_Iraq" title="List of largest cities of Iraq">Iraqi cities</a> and it is also used as the standard dialect in music and formal speech.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some modern Hakkari speakers from Iraq can switch <a href="/wiki/Accent_reduction" title="Accent reduction">back and forth</a> from their Hakkari dialects to Iraqi Koine when conversing with Assyrian speakers of other dialects. Some <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Syria" title="Assyrians in Syria">Syrian-Assyrians</a>, who originate from Hakkari, may also speak or sing in Iraqi Koine. This is attributed to the growing exposure to Assyrian Standard-based literature, media and its use as a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical</a> language by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a>, which is based in Iraq. Elements of original <i>Ashiret</i> dialects can still be observed in Iraqi Koine, especially in that of older speakers. Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_folk/pop_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian folk/pop music">Assyrian songs</a> are generally sung in Iraqi Koine in order for them to be intelligible and have widespread recognition. To note, the emergence of Koine did not signify that the rest of the spoken dialects vanished. The <i>Ashiret</i> dialects are still active today and widely spoken in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and northeastern Syria as some Assyrians remained in the rural areas and the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Immigrant_generations" title="Immigrant generations">first generation</a> speakers who relocated in urban areas still maintained their native dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect_continuum">Dialect continuum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Dialect continuum"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neo-Aramaic has a rather slightly defined <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, starting from the Assyrians in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">Alqosh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batnaya" title="Batnaya">Batnaya</a>) and ending with those in Western <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (<a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>). The dialects in Northern Iraq, such as those of Alqosh and Batnaya, would be minimally unintelligible to those in Western Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-Shamāshā_Dāwīd_1963,_p._895_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shamāshā_Dāwīd_1963,_p._895-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nearing the Iraqi-Turkey border, the <a href="/wiki/Barwari" title="Barwari">Barwari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">Tyari</a> dialects are more "traditionally Assyrian" and would sound like those in the <a href="/wiki/Hakkari_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakkari province">Hakkari province</a> in Turkey. Furthermore, the Barwar and Tyari dialects are "transitional", acquiring both Assyrian and Chaldean phonetic features (though they do not use /ħ/). Gawar, Diz and <a href="/wiki/Jilu_(tribe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jilu (tribe)">Jilu</a> are in the "centre" of the spectrum, which lie halfway between Tyari and Urmia, having features of both respective dialects, though still being distinct in their own manner.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Hakkari, going east (towards <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Nochiya_(tribe)" title="Nochiya (tribe)">Nochiya</a> dialect would begin to sound distinct to the Tyari/Barwar dialects and more like the Urmian dialect in <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Azerbaijan_province" title="West Azerbaijan province">West Azerbaijan province</a>, containing a few Urmian features. The Urmian dialect, alongside Iraqi Koine, are considered to be "Standard Assyrian", though Iraqi Koine is more widespread and has thus become the more common standard dialect in recent times. Both Koine and Urmian share phonetic characteristics with the Nochiya dialect to some degree.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">Syriac literature</a></div>
<p>Early Syriac texts still date to the 2nd century, notably the <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Syriac Bible</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Diatesseron" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatesseron">Diatesseron</a></i> Gospel harmony. The bulk of Syriac literary production dates to between the 4th and 8th centuries.
Classical Syriac literacy survives into the 9th century, though Syriac Christian authors in this period increasingly wrote in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>. The emergence of spoken <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">Neo-Aramaic</a> is conventionally dated to the 13th century, but a number of authors continued producing literary works in Syriac in the later medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrock1996_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrock1996-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because Assyrian, alongside <a href="/wiki/Turoyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Turoyo">Turoyo</a>, is the most widely spoken variety of Syriac today, modern Syriac literature would therefore usually be written in those varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The conversion of the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> to Islam began a period of retreat and hardship for <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> and its adherents, although there still has been a continuous stream of Syriac literature in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> from the 14th century through to the present day. This has included the flourishing of literature from the various colloquial <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic</a> <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">Neo-Aramaic</a> languages still spoken by <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>.
</p><p>This <i>Neo-Syriac</i> literature bears a dual tradition: it continues the traditions of the Syriac literature of the past and it incorporates a converging stream of the less homogeneous spoken language. The first such flourishing of Neo-Syriac was the seventeenth century literature of the School of <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">Alqosh</a>, in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> This literature led to the establishment of Assyrian Aramaic as written literary languages.
</p><p>In the nineteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing presses</a> were established in <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>, in northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. This led to the establishment of the 'General Urmian' dialect of Assyrian Neo-Aramaic as the standard in much Neo-Syriac Assyrian literature up until the 20th century. The <i>Urmia Bible</i>, published in 1852 by Justin Perkins was based on the <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a>, where it included a parallel translation in the Urmian dialect. The comparative ease of modern publishing methods has encouraged other colloquial Neo-Aramaic languages, like Turoyo, to begin to produce literature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrock1992_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrock1992-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrock2006_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrock2006-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suret_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: External links"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1134653256"><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Incubator-logo.svg/32px-Incubator-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Incubator-logo.svg/48px-Incubator-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Incubator-logo.svg/64px-Incubator-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="1000" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><i><b><a href="https://incubator.wikimedia.org/wiki/Wp/aii" class="extiw" title="incubator:Wp/aii">Suret language test</a></b></i> of <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia" title="Wikipedia">Wikipedia</a> at <a href="https://meta.wikimedia.org/wiki/Wikimedia_Incubator" class="extiw" title="meta:Wikimedia Incubator">Wikimedia Incubator</a></div></div>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1134653256"><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">For a list of words relating to Suret language, see the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:Suret_language" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:Category:Suret language"><i><b>Suret language</b></i> category of words</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wiktionary" title="Wiktionary">Wiktionary</a>, the free dictionary.</div></div>
